Polishing a car's paint by hand?  Wow, I didn't know that could be done, I thought it could only ever be done using a machine.  Said by the kid in the video that never heard of anyone polishing a car by hand before.   LOL somehow I'd bet he has never used a toothbrush that wasn't battery powered.  The word "manual" refers more than just transmissions.  I still remember the day when my friend bought a 1966 Ford Falcon, all black, the paint was as hard as glass.  When he first showed it to us it could have past for gray, but after polishing it by hand for hours it ended up looking like the day it rolled off the line.  We had no paint decontamination products back in the late 80s other than soap, and all we used were rags, an old bath towel ripped into chunks.  We did have the same chrome rust remover that is still used today, that completely restored the chrome.  It did have one dent that his father knocked out with a few blocks of wood and a tire jack to do the pushing from the inside.  To completely finish everything the paint, engine, wheels, chrome, replace the anttinia, and deage the rubber molding around windows took 80% of a weekend with 3 people working on it by hand, nothing but elbow grease, manually.  btw that RX7 came out amazing, other when a few harmless mice & some paint swirls it looked like an easy job for you guys, if it was sold, how much did it go for?

A little bit of history; The most popular car with a Mazda rotary engine that sent the rest of the models to the history dump. In the period from 1978 to 2002, 3 generations were released. There were variations of the layout: front mid-engine and rear-wheel drive. The year 1991 became an epoch-making one of its kind, as a version of the car with the 13B-REW engine was released, which was the first serial twin-turbo unit supplied from Japan to other countries.

So Mazda made three versions of the RX7 in this model and years.  I was selling them when they first came out.  There was the base model, a race version and a touring version. This one is the racing version because it has the red stabilizer under the hood, mounted between the shock towers. The roof design was unique for aerodynamics. Unlike other RX7s there were no convertibles, only hard tops.  It was a Rotary engine with turbo charge. Zero to Sixty in 4.5 seconds. The touring version had a Bose stereo system and automatic transmission, all others were manual five speed.  Estimated value with those miles, 40K. Awesome find, these are very rare !

First of all congratulations on this awesome find.  Make sure you go over all the seals, the tires are most likely dry rotted and need replacement.  What about the seals in the calipers.  The Wankel motor was notorious in those days, make sure the engine is flushed twice to get the sediment out.  Fuel system clean.   This paint must be in great factory condition.  So to the value of this car, there are not many with this history and paperwork so it is pretty rare.  In this condition, and with all the mechanical issues sorted, it is most likely going to be anywhere from 68,000 - 75,000 depending if you find a collector.  I wouldn't be surprised if you pulled it over the Barrett Jackson auction it might fetch a little more.  The car is completely original and stock, no scrapes, dents or any damage I assume, I did not see any when you were detailing.  Please keep this vehicle off winter roads with salt, the Mazdas from these era can be rust buckets.

I don't know how many of those I had apart over the years. The vacuum system was so complicated underneath that intake manifold that most of the time hoses were rearranged and causing drivability issues. It didn't help that there was a recall on the turbocharger because most technicians were inexperienced and created more problems than they solved. The biggest complaint on that model was people didn't like how uncomfortable they were on the road and were always coming in and asking if there was a way to give it a softer ride. Of course we always told them no. They come in regularly in need of a new engine but typically upon disassembly they would need a turbo and an exhaust manifold due to cracks. Even in '90s dollars it was generally close to $20,000 out the door.
After such a long time, Tires, brakes, all liquids, all rubber hoses like fuel and break hoses, all filters, windscreen wipers etc are dead and must be replaced. otherwise this car is a rolling coffin. And for god sake, you never ever clean the engine with a high pressure cleaner ğŸ¤¦ğŸ¼â€â™‚ï¸

This was my dream car.  Driving back from my junior year in college in 1978, I stopped by the Santa Barbara, CA Mazda dealership with a friend who drove the the RX-4.  There in the showroom was the first model year of the RX-7.  I fell and love and worked a deal with a friend in the car leasing business for a red, 4 speed.  I purchased eventually for $5500 and later sold it for $8,000.   Always hoped I be able to purchase another.  The model that was last tagged in 1999 was probably the last model year (1995).  It was a gorgeous design with a great power train complimented I think by twin turbos easily capable of reaching 140 mph.  The 1978 version had a power plant that overmatched the suspension.  I reached 120 and felt like I was floating.  What a beauty.

1995 was the last production year for the RX-7 in North America. This is either a 94 or 95 with the twin turbo charged twin rotor 13reb engine cranking out ~265 hp @ 4000 rpm. It was a fast car for sure. Problem was the Wankel rotary engine had problems meeting emissions standards so Mazda pulled them off the US market even though they continued to be sold in Japan until 2002. They sold for $40k equipped like this one in 1995. This car you have is practically cherry. On a good day at auction $100-$120k probably.
